import torch
from transformers import AutoModelForCausalLM, AutoConfig

class Model(torch.nn.Module):
    def __init__(self, model_name, config):
        super().__init__()
        self.model_name = model_name
        self.config = config
        self.model = AutoModelForCausalLM.from_pretrained(self.model_name, config=self.config)

    def forward(self, x):
        return self.model(x).logits

model_name = "gpt2"
config = AutoConfig.from_pretrained(model_name)
vocab_size = config.vocab_size
sequence_length = 256
batch_size = 32

def get_inputs():
    inputs = torch.randint(0, vocab_size, (batch_size, sequence_length))
    return [inputs]

def get_init_inputs():
    return [model_name, config]

With 256 queries and 64 head dimension: - Load K tile [256, 64] into shared memory: 256*64*4 bytes = 65KB. That's too much for shared memory (usually 48KB max per block)! - Wait, shared memory is 48KB on most GPUs. 65KB is too much. - We can load smaller tiles. E.g., load K tile of [64, 64] = 16KB. - Actually, for K we need all 256 keys to compute one query's attention scores. But we only need to load K/V tile at a time. FlashAttention algorithm: 1. Tile over the sequence dimension 2. For each tile, compute partial attention scores 3. Online softmax to accumulate But writing FlashAttention from scratch is very hard to get right. Since T=256 is small, what if I compute the attention per head using a simple approach where I stream through K/V in shared memory tiles? For one query, I need to compute dot product with all 256 keys. I can load keys in tiles of, say, 64. For each tile: - Load K_tile [64, 64] to shared memory - Load V_tile [64, 64] to shared memory - Compute Q_i @ K_tile^T → scores for 64 keys - Track running max and sum for softmax - Accumulate weighted sum Wait, I need to do this for all queries at once to be efficient. Or I could have one block handle a group of queries. Actually, for T=256 and D=64, each block could handle 4 queries (since 4*64 = 256, and 4*256 scores = 1024 floats = 4KB). Let me think... Actually, the simplest approach that might be competitive: - One block per (batch, head) - Load all of K and V into shared memory... but 256*64*4*2 = 131KB, too big. - So we must tile. What if we process the sequence in chunks? For each chunk of keys: - Load K_chunk and V_chunk into shared memory - For each query (or group of queries), compute partial attention - Track softmax stats online This is essentially FlashAttention but simpler because T is small. I think the key insight is: **With T=256, we might actually get decent performance even with a relatively naive attention kernel**, because the memory footprint of the attention matrices ([256, 256] per head) is small enough that even if we write them out to global memory, it's not terrible. So perhaps the best approach is: 1. For attention: use PyTorch GEMMs for QKV and output projection, but fuse scale + mask + softmax into one custom kernel, and do the two attention matmuls via PyTorch. 2. For MLP: fuse bias_add + GELU into one custom kernel. 3. For transformer blocks: fuse residual adds with layer norms where possible. 4. Implement the whole thing manually to avoid Transformers overhead. Actually, I just realized that PyTorch's `torch.matmul` for [B, H, T, D] @ [B, H, D, T] is already quite efficient (it calls batched GEMM from cuBLAS). For T=256, D=64, the Q@K^T is 384 batched GEMMs of [256, 64] @ [64, 256]. cuBLAS handles this well. Similarly for Attn@V. So the bottleneck might not be the attention matmuls themselves, but the elementwise ops around them. Let me identify all the small kernels in one transformer layer: **Attention path:** 1. Linear c_attn (GEMM) 2. Split + reshape (view ops, no kernel) 3. Q @ K^T (GEMM) 4. Scale (elementwise) 5. Add causal mask (elementwise) 6. Softmax (kernel) 7. attn @ V (GEMM) 8. Reshape + merge (view ops) 9. Linear c_proj (GEMM) 10. Residual add (elementwise) 11. LayerNorm (kernel) **MLP path:** 12. LayerNorm (kernel) 13. Linear c_fc (GEMM) 14. Bias add (elementwise... actually included in linear if using bias=True) 15. GELU (elementwise) 16. Linear c_proj (GEMM) 17. Residual add (elementwise) And final LN + LM head: 18. LayerNorm 19. LM Head (GEMM) So per layer, the small kernels are: scale, mask, softmax, residual add, layer norm, (another layer norm), GELU, residual add. That's about 8 small kernels per layer, times 12 = 96 kernel launches. Plus 12 * 4 GEMMs = 48 GEMM calls. I can fuse: - Scale + Mask + Softmax → 1 kernel (saves 2 kernels per layer, 24 total) - Residual add + LayerNorm → 1 kernel (saves 1 kernel per layer for the attention path, 12 total) - Bias add + GELU → 1 kernel? Well, linear in PyTorch already includes bias, so this is fused. But wait, if we use `torch.nn.functional.linear`, the bias add is done in the same cuBLAS call or a separate kernel. Actually, PyTorch's linear with bias typically calls the GEMM and then a bias add kernel (or fuses it if possible). For small matrices, it might be a separate kernel. - MLP output residual add + next layer's LN → 1 kernel? No, the MLP output is `x = residual + mlp(x)`, then next layer does `ln(x)`. But we also need the intermediate for the next attention. Actually, `residual + mlp_out` and then `ln` — we can fuse residual add + ln for the next block's first ln. Wait, let me trace through more carefully. In a standard transformer block: ```python attn_input = ln_1(x) attn_out = attn(attn_input) x = x + attn_out mlp_input = ln_2(x) mlp_out = mlp(mlp_input) x = x + mlp_out ``` So fusions possible: 1. `ln_1` is standalone (input is from previous block or embedding) 2. `attn` internally: QKV linear → attention → proj linear → attn_output 3. `x + attn_out` is standalone 4. `ln_2` is standalone 5. `mlp` internally: fc → gelu → proj 6. `x + mlp_out` is standalone For the next block, `ln_1` takes `x + mlp_out` as input. So we could fuse step 6 and the next block's step 1! That is, compute `x = x + mlp_out`, then immediately do `ln_1(x)` in the same kernel. Similarly, after the last block, we have `ln_f` and then `lm_head`. Can we fuse `ln_f + lm_head`? The linear layer needs properly formatted LN output. In theory we can do LN and then call GEMM. If we fuse to a single kernel, the kernel would do LN and then... it can't call cuBLAS. So no. What about fusing residual add + LN? Yes, we can write a kernel that does: ```python x = x + residual x = layer_norm(x, weight, bias, epsilon) ``` This saves one kernel launch and one round-trip to memory. What about fusing attention's scale + causal mask + softmax? Yes, one kernel that reads the attention scores, scales, applies causal mask, and writes softmax output. This saves memory bandwidth. What about fusing GELU with something? After `fc` linear, we have: `[B, T, 4C]`. The linear is a GEMM. GELU is elementwise. PyTorch's `torch.nn.functional.linear` does GEMM + bias add (fused in cuBLAS for some sizes, separate for others). Then GELU is a separate kernel. We can write a kernel that computes `gelu(linear(x, W, b))`. But we can't easily do the linear part in CUDA without a GEMM implementation. Wait, unless we use the fact that the weight is known and call `torch.matmul` for the GEMM, then a custom kernel for bias+gelu. That is: ```python fc_out = torch.matmul(x, c_fc_weight.t()) fc_out = custom_bias_gelu(fc_out, c_fc_bias) ``` This custom kernel does elementwise bias add + GELU in one pass. Saves one kernel launch and memory bandwidth. Similarly for the attention softmax: ```python attn_scores = torch.matmul(query, key.transpose(-1, -2)) attn_probs = custom_scaled_masked_softmax(attn_scores, causal_mask, scale) attn_output = torch.matmul(attn_probs, value) ``` Saves scale, mask, and softmax as separate kernel launches. OK so let's sketch the kernels I'll need: 1. `fused_residual_ln(x, residual, weight, bias)` → `ln(x + residual)` 2. `fused_bias_gelu(x, bias)` → `gelu(x + bias)` 3. `fused_scaled_masked_softmax(x, mask, scale)` → `softmax(x * scale + mask)` And I can implement the rest with PyTorch GEMMs. Wait, for the causal mask in GPT-2, it's typically a constant lower-triangular mask of -inf (or -10000). Instead of passing it every time, I can just compute it in the kernel since it's purely based on causal positions. For position `i, j`, if `j > i`, mask = -inf. This saves even more memory bandwidth! So kernel #3 becomes `fused_scaled_causal_softmax(x, scale)`. That's even better. Let me also consider if I should fuse the final `residual + mlp_out` with the next layer's `ln_1`. For 12 layers, I can have 11 fusions and 1 standalone at the end. Actually, let me structure it as: ```python # First layer h = self.wte(input_ids) + self.wpe(positions) h = fused_residual_ln(h, torch.zeros_like(h), ln_1_weight, ln_1_bias) # Just LN, no residual ... # Inside loop: for layer in self.layers: # h is already layer-normalized from previous fused_residual_ln? # No, let me restructure. ``` Actually, let me think about this differently. The standard flow is: ``` h = embed for each layer: residual = h h = layer_norm_1(residual) attn_out = attention(h) h = residual + attn_out residual = h h = layer_norm_2(residual) mlp_out = mlp(h) h = residual + mlp_out h = final_layer_norm(h) logits = lm_head(h) ``` I can restructure the computation to be: ```python h = embed for i, layer in enumerate(self.layers): # h is the raw hidden state from previous layer # Step 1: attn_input = ln_1(h) # Step 2: attn_out = attention(attn_input) # Step 3: h = h + attn_out # We can fuse steps 1, 2, 3 partially... # Actually, attention takes ln_1(h) as input, not h. # And attention returns attn_out. # Then h_new = h + attn_out # Then mlp_input = ln_2(h_new) # For the first part: compute attn_out = attention(ln_1(h)) # Then h = h + attn_out # Then compute mlp_out = mlp(ln_2(h)) # Then h = h + mlp_out # Can we fuse: h + attn_out → ln_2(h) ? Yes! # So after attention: # h = fused_residual_ln(h, attn_out, ln_2_weight, ln_2_bias) # Then mlp_out = mlp(h) # Then for the next iteration, we need h + mlp_out → ln_1_next # So: h = fused_residual_ln(h, mlp_out, next_ln_1_weight, next_ln_1_bias) ``` Wait, that means each layer becomes: 1. `attn_input = ln_1(h)` (unless h was already normalized from previous fused op) 2. `attn_out = attention(attn_input)` 3. `h = fused_residual_ln(h, attn_out, ln_2_weight, ln_2_bias)` 4. `mlp_out = mlp(h)` 5. `h = fused_residual_ln(h, mlp_out, next_ln_1_weight, next_ln_1_bias)` For the first layer, there's no previous normalization, so we do `h = ln_1(h)` then attention. For the last layer, after step 5, `h` is normalized for... nothing. Actually after the last MLP, we do `h = h + mlp_out`, then `final_ln(h)`. So for the last layer, step 5 should use no next_ln_1. Instead we do `h = h + mlp_out`, then later `h = final_ln(h)`. Hmm, this gets complicated because each layer has two layer norms: - ln_1 is before attention - ln_2 is before MLP The residual connection is after attention and after MLP. For layer i (except layer 0): - Input to layer i: `h` = output of previous layer's MLP + residual (unnormalized) - `attn_input = ln_1_i(h)` - `attn_out = attn_i(attn_input)` - `h = h + attn_out` → this is unnormalized - `mlp_input = ln_2_i(h)` - `mlp_out = mlp_i(mlp_input)` - `h = h + mlp_out` → this is the output of layer i So the natural fusions are: - `residual + attn_out` is standalone - `ln_2(residual + attn_out)` - We can't easily fuse these two because attention output is computed asynchronously. But we CAN write: ```python attn_input = ln_1(h) attn_out = attn(attn_input) h = h + attn_out h = ln_2(h) mlp_out = mlp(h) h = h + mlp_out ``` Fusing to: ```python attn_input = ln_1(h) attn_out = attn(attn_input) h = fused_add_ln(h, attn_out, ln_2_gamma, ln_2_beta) # h = ln_2(h + attn_out) mlp_out = mlp(h) h = fused_add_ln(h, mlp_out, ln_1_next_gamma, ln_1_next_beta) # h = ln_1_next(h + mlp_out), if there is a next layer ``` For the last layer: ```python attn_input = ln_1(h) attn_out = attn(attn_input) h = fused_add_ln(h, attn_out, ln_2_gamma, ln_2_beta) mlp_out = mlp(h) h = h + mlp_out h = final_ln(h) ``` So each layer uses: - 1 ln_1 (unless fused from previous) - 1 attention - 1 fused_add_ln (combining residual after attn + ln_2) - 1 mlp - 1 fused_add_ln or add (for MLP residual) This saves 2 ln calls (we fuse add + ln), so 2 kernel launches saved per layer. For 12 layers, that's 24 saved. Plus in MLP, if we fuse bias + GELU, that saves 1 per layer = 12 saved. Plus in attention, if we fuse scale + causal mask + softmax, that saves 2 per layer = 24 saved. Total saved: ~60 kernel launches. That could be significant! OK let me now think about kernel implementations. ### Kernel 1: fused_add_ln ```cuda __global__ void fused_add_ln_kernel( float* out, const float* residual, const float* delta, const float* gamma, const float* beta, int B, int T, int C, float eps ) { // Each block handles one (b, t) position // Compute x = residual[b,t,:] + delta[b,t,:] // Compute mean and variance // Compute ln = (x - mean) / sqrt(var + eps) * gamma + beta // Write to out[b,t,:] } ``` This is straightforward. Shared memory for reduction. ### Kernel 2: fused_bias_gelu ```cuda __global__ void fused_bias_gelu_kernel( float* out, const float* inp, const float* bias, int N, int C ) { // For each element: x = inp[i] + bias[col] // out[i] = x * 0.5 * (1.0 + erf(x / sqrt(2.0))) } ``` ### Kernel 3: fused_scaled_causal_softmax ```cuda __global__ void fused_scaled_causal_softmax_kernel( float* out, const float* inp, float scale, int B, int H, int T ) { // inp and out are [B, H, T, T] // For each (b, h, row): // Load row inp[b,h,row,:] // For col > row: set to -inf (or large negative) // Scale by scale // Compute max // Compute exp(x - max) // Compute sum // Write softmax } ``` This is a row-wise softmax with causal masking. Each block handles one row. T=256, so 256 elements per row. Easy to parallelize with shared memory for reduction. For correctness, causal mask means `col > row` gets set to a large negative number (like -1e9 or -10000.0). In the reference, GPT2 uses a registered buffer `bias` which is a causal mask of -1e4 or something. Let me check the exact value. In GPT2 code: ```python self.register_buffer( "bias", torch.tril(torch.ones((self.config.max_position_embeddings, self.config.max_position_embeddings), dtype=torch.bool)).view(1, 1, self.config.max_position_embeddings, self.config.max_position_embeddings), persistent=False ) ``` And in `_attn`: ```python attn_weights = torch.where(self.bias, attn_weights, self.masked_bias) ``` Where `self.masked_bias` is a register parameter initialized to `-1e4`: ```python self.register_parameter("masked_bias", None) self.masked_bias = torch.tensor(-1e4) ``` Wait, in newer transformers it might be different. Let me check. Actually, `self.masked_bias` is typically `-1e4` as a scalar. Let me use `-1e4` or `-10000.0f`. For numerical equivalence, I need to match exactly. HuggingFace applies causal mask BEFORE softmax, and uses `torch.where(causal_mask, attn_weights, -1e4)`. So my kernel should: for positions where `col > row`, set to `-1e4`. Also, the scaling factor is `1.0 / sqrt(head_dim)` where head_dim = 64. So scale = `1.0 / 8.0 = 0.125`. Wait, let me double check. In GPT2 attention: `attn_weights = attn_weights / (float(value.size(-1)) ** 0.5)`. `value.size(-1)` is head_dim = 768/12 = 64. `sqrt(64) = 8`. So yes, divide by 8. Actually, I also need to check if GPT2 uses additive attention or multiplicative? The causal mask is boolean `True` for allowed, `False` for masked. And `torch.where(mask, values, -1e4)`. So yes. Now, for PyTorch's softmax vs my kernel's softmax, I need to make sure I compute it correctly to get numerical equivalence within fp32 tolerance. Softmax of row x: ```python m = x.max(dim=-1, keepdim=True) e = exp(x - m) s = e.sum(dim=-1, keepdim=True) out = e / s ``` I need to use the same numerically-stable approach. Also, there's an important detail: the reference GPT2 uses float32 for logits computation even if weights are in other formats (the task says fp32 precision). So I should use float32 in my kernels. Now, let me think about the overall structure of `ModelNew`: ```python class ModelNew(torch.nn.Module): def __init__(self, model_name, config): super().__init__() self.model_name = model_name self.config = config # Load reference model to extract weights ref_model = AutoModelForCausalLM.from_pretrained(model_name, config=config) ref_model.eval() # Extract transformer weights transformer = ref_model.transformer self.wte = transformer.wte.weight.detach() self.wpe = transformer.wpe.weight.detach() self.layers = [] for i, block in enumerate(transformer.h): layer_params = { 'ln_1_g': block.ln_1.weight.detach(), 'ln_1_b': block.ln_1.bias.detach(), 'attn_c_attn_w': block.attn.c_attn.weight.detach(), 'attn_c_attn_b': block.attn.c_attn.bias.detach(), 'attn_c_proj_w': block.attn.c_proj.weight.detach(), 'attn_c_proj_b': block.attn.c_proj.bias.detach(), 'ln_2_g': block.ln_2.weight.detach(), 'ln_2_b': block.ln_2.bias.detach(), 'mlp_c_fc_w': block.mlp.c_fc.weight.detach(), 'mlp_c_fc_b': block.mlp.c_fc.bias.detach(), 'mlp_c_proj_w': block.mlp.c_proj.weight.detach(), 'mlp_c_proj_b': block.mlp.c_proj.bias.detach(), } self.layers.append(layer_params) self.ln_f_g = transformer.ln_f.weight.detach() self.ln_f_b = transformer.ln_f.bias.detach() self.lm_head_w = ref_model.lm_head.weight.detach() # Register buffers/params ... ``` Wait, for the attention weights. In GPT2, `c_attn` is a `Conv1D` layer. In newer `transformers`, it might be `nn.Linear`. Let me check. In older `transformers`, GPT2 uses `Conv1D` which stores weight as `[in_features, out_features]` instead of `[out_features, in_features]`. The actual computation is `x @ weight + bias` where `x` is [..., in_features] and `weight` is [in_features, out_features]. In `nn.Linear`, the computation is `x @ weight.T + bias` where `weight` is [out_features, in_features]. So I need to be careful about whether to transpose. Let me check what `Conv1D` does: ```python class Conv1D(nn.Module): def __init__(self, nf, nx): super().__init__() self.nf = nf self.weight = nn.Parameter(torch.empty(nx, nf)) self.bias = nn.Parameter(torch.zeros(nf)) def forward(self, x): size_out = x.size()[:-1] + (self.nf,) x = torch.addmm(self.bias, x.view(-1, x.size(-1)), self.weight) return x.view(size_out) ``` So yes, `Conv1D` stores weight as `[nx, nf]` and does `x @ weight + bias` (with addmm which is `bias + x.matmul(weight)`). If newer transformers use `Linear`, it would be transposed. I'll check the shapes when extracting. To be safe, I should call the layer and check the output shape, or look at the module type. But actually, I can just use the actual weights and call `torch.nn.functional.linear` or `torch.matmul` as appropriate. Actually, since I'm extracting the weights directly, I can just check if the module is `Conv1D` or `Linear` and handle accordingly. Let me figure out what modern `transformers` uses. Looking at GPT2 source: the `Conv1D` class is defined in `transformers.pytorch_utils`. GPT2 still uses it in recent versions. So: - `c_attn`: `[768, 2304]` weight - `c_proj`: `[768, 768]` weight - `c_fc`: `[768, 3072]` weight - `c_proj` (mlp): `[3072, 768]` weight And the computation is `x @ W + b` where x is [..., 768]. In PyTorch, this is equivalent to `F.linear(x, W.T, b)`. Because `F.linear(x, W_lin, b)` does `x @ W_lin.T + b`. So if `W_conv1d` is [in, out], then `W_lin = W_conv1d.T` is [out, in], and `F.linear(x, W_lin, b)` gives the same result. So I can transpose all Conv1D weights to use `F.linear`, or just use `torch.matmul(x, W) + b`. I'll use `torch.matmul(x, W) + b` for simplicity. Wait, but `torch.matmul` for 2D with 3D? If x is [B, T, C] and W is [C, D], then `x @ W` works and gives [B, T, D]. Yes. OK, now let me think about how to structure the Python code and CUDA source. ### Python Code Structure I'll define `ModelNew` with an `__init__` that creates all the parameters as registered buffers or parameters. Then in `forward`, I'll call my custom extension functions for the fusions. I'll use `torch.utils.cpp_extension.load_inline` with `cuda_sources` containing the CUDA kernels, and `functions` listing the host wrappers. The host wrappers will look like: ```cpp torch::Tensor fused_add_ln_cuda(torch::Tensor residual, torch::Tensor delta, torch::Tensor gamma, torch::Tensor beta, float eps); torch::Tensor fused_bias_gelu_cuda(torch::Tensor input, torch::Tensor bias); torch::Tensor fused_scaled_causal_softmax_cuda(torch::Tensor input, float scale); ``` Actually, looking at how `load_inline` works, I need to write host wrapper functions that take `torch::Tensor` and return `torch::Tensor`. And `functions=['fused_add_ln_cuda', 'fused_bias_gelu_cuda', 'fused_scaled_causal_softmax_cuda']`. ### CUDA Kernel Details **fused_add_ln_cuda:** Input: `residual` [B, T, C], `delta` [B, T, C], `gamma` [C], `beta` [C] Output: `out` [B, T, C] ```cuda __global__ void fused_add_ln_kernel(float* out, const float* residual, const float* delta, const float* gamma, const float* beta, int B, int T, int C, float eps) { int bt = blockIdx.x; // Each block one (b, t) int b = bt / T; int t = bt % T; if (b >= B) return; int offset = (b * T + t) * C; const float* r_ptr = residual + offset; const float* d_ptr = delta + offset; float* o_ptr = out + offset; // Load into shared memory or compute directly // Use shared memory for reduction extern __shared__ float sdata[]; // Compute x = r + d float sum = 0.0f; float sq_sum = 0.0f; for (int c = threadIdx.x; c < C; c += blockDim.x) { float x = r_ptr[c] + d_ptr[c]; sdata[c] = x; // Store temporarily if we have enough shared mem sum += x; sq_sum += x * x; } __syncthreads(); // Reduce sum and sq_sum // ... float mean = sum / C; float var = sq_sum / C - mean * mean; float inv_std = rsqrtf(var + eps); for (int c = threadIdx.x; c < C; c += blockDim.x) { float x = r_ptr[c] + d_ptr[c]; // or load from shared mem o_ptr[c] = (x - mean) * inv_std * gamma[c] + beta[c]; } } ``` If C=768, using shared memory for the entire row: 768*4=3072 bytes. Easily fits. Each block handles one (b,t). Total blocks = B*T = 32*256 = 8192. That should work fine. Block size = 256 threads or something. But wait, for the reduction, if I use shared memory for the whole row, I can store the row in shared mem, then do tree reduction for sum. Let me write it properly. Actually, there's a simpler approach for small C=768: - Use `__shared__ float s_sum[1]` and `__shared__ float s_sq_sum[1]` - Each thread computes partial sum of its elements - Do atomicAdd or tree reduction to `s_sum[0]` and `s_sq_sum[0]` - `__syncthreads()` - Then each thread writes its output Wait, tree reduction is cleaner. But for simplicity, I could have each thread loop over a subset and write to shared memory array of size blockDim.x, then reduce: ```cuda __shared__ float s_mean[1]; __shared__ float s_var[1]; // Step 1: compute partial sums float thread_sum = 0; float thread_sq = 0; for (int c = threadIdx.x; c < C; c += blockDim.x) { float x = r_ptr[c] + d_ptr[c]; thread_sum += x; thread_sq += x * x; } // Step 2: reduce within block __shared__ float s_sum[256]; __shared__ float s_sq[256]; s_sum[threadIdx.x] = thread_sum; s_sq[threadIdx.x] = thread_sq; __syncthreads(); // Tree reduction for (int stride = blockDim.x / 2; stride > 0; stride >>= 1) { if (threadIdx.x < stride) { s_sum[threadIdx.x] += s_sum[threadIdx.x + stride]; s_sq[threadIdx.x] += s_sq[threadIdx.x + stride]; } __syncthreads(); } if (threadIdx.x == 0) { float mean = s_sum[0] / C; float var = s_sq[0] / C - mean * mean; s_mean[0] = mean; s_var[0] = var; } __syncthreads(); float mean = s_mean[0]; float inv_std = rsqrtf(s_var[0] + eps); // Step 3: write output for (int c = threadIdx.x; c < C; c += blockDim.x) { float x = r_ptr[c] + d_ptr[c]; o_ptr[c] = (x - mean) * inv_std * gamma[c] + beta[c]; } ``` Block size of 256 is fine. With C=768, each thread handles exactly 3 elements (768/256=3). So the loop `c += blockDim.x` is not even needed! Well, 768/256 = 3 exactly. Nice. Actually, if blockDim.x is 256, threadIdx.x ranges 0..255. For C=768: - thread 0: c=0, 256, 512 - thread 1: c=1, 257, 513 - ... - thread 255: c=255, 511, 767 Perfect, each thread handles exactly 3 elements. For the tree reduction, with 256 threads, we reduce to 1. This is efficient. **fused_bias_gelu_cuda:** ```cuda __global__ void fused_bias_gelu_kernel(float* out, const float* inp, const float* bias, int B, int T, int C) { int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int total = B * T * C; if (idx >= total) return; int c = idx % C; float x = inp[idx] + bias[c]; // GELU: x * 0.5 * (1 + tanh(sqrt(2/pi) * (x + 0.044715 * x^3))) // or x * 0.5 * (1 + erf(x / sqrt(2))) // PyTorch uses the tanh approximation for speed and numerical stability // Let me check what PyTorch GELU uses internally. ``` Wait, what formula does PyTorch use for GELU? Let me check. PyTorch's GELU uses: ```cpp // from PyTorch source static inline float GeluActivation(float x) { // Actual implementation: // 0.5 * x * (1 + erf(x / sqrt(2))) } ``` However, there's also the approximate version. By default, `torch.nn.functional.gelu` uses `approximate='none'` which computes via `erf`. But wait, in `transformers`, GPT2 uses `nn.functional.gelu`: In GPT2 config, `activation_function = "gelu_new"` or `"gelu"`. Let me check. GPT-2 uses `gelu_new` in older configs? Actually, standard GPT-2 uses GELU. Wait, in the `GPT2MLP`: ```python self.act = ACT2FN[config.activation_function] ``` And `config.activation_function` for GPT2 is `"gelu_new"` in older versions? Let me check the actual GPT-2 config. Actually `gelu_new` refers to the `gelu_new` activation in transformers which is a specific implementation. Let me check: ```python def gelu_new(x): return 0.5 * x * (1.0 + torch.tanh(math.sqrt(2.0 / math.pi) * (x + 0.044715 * torch.pow(x, 3.0)))) ``` Actually, I should check the `config.activation_function`. For GPT2, it's "gelu_new" in many model configs. But the base `gpt2` might use standard `gelu` or `gelu_new`. Wait, let me look this up. The original GPT-2 used standard GELU with the `tanh` approximation? Or with `erf`? The original paper used the `erf` version. However, in `transformers`, there's a function called `gelu_new` which is what OpenAI actually used. From `transformers.activations`: ```python def gelu_new(input): return 0.5 * input * (1.0 + torch.tanh(math.sqrt(2.0 / math.pi) * (input + 0.044715 * torch.pow(input, 3.0)))) ``` And GPT2's default is `gelu_new`. I need to verify this! In `transformers.models.gpt2.configuration_gpt2`: ```python activation_function = "gelu_new" ``` Yes, for GPT2, the default is `gelu_new`. So I need to use the `gelu_new` formula: ```python 0.5 * x * (1.0 + tanh(sqrt(2/pi) * (x + 0.044715 * x^3))) ``` In C++: ```cpp float gelu_new(float x) { return 0.5f * x * (1.0f + tanhf(0.7978845608f * (x + 0.044715f * x * x * x))); } ``` Where 0.7978845608 = sqrt(2/pi). I need to be careful to match exactly what PyTorch does. Actually, there's a subtlety: PyTorch's `tanh` and C++ `tanhf` should match for float32 inputs. And the constants need to be represented accurately. `0.7978845608028654` is more precise. And `0.044715`. For correctness, let me use these precise values. **fused_scaled_causal_softmax_cuda:** Input: `scores` [B, H, T, T] Output: `probs` [B, H, T, T] Param: `scale` (float) ```cuda __global__ void fused_scaled_causal_softmax_kernel( float* out, const float* inp, float scale, int B, int H, int T ) { // One block per row int b = blockIdx.x / (H * T); int h = (blockIdx.x / T) % H; int t = blockIdx.x % T; if (b >= B) return; int row_offset = ((b * H + h) * T + t) * T; const float* row_inp = inp + row_offset; float* row_out = out + row_offset; // Find max float local_max = -1e20f; for (int c = threadIdx.x; c <= t; c += blockDim.x) { float val = row_inp[c] * scale; if (val > local_max) local_max = val; } __shared__ float s_max; // Block reduction for max // ... // Then compute softmax } ``` Wait, causal masking means `col > row` should be set to a very negative number. Since we're iterating `c <= t` (where `t` is the row index), we only process valid causal positions. For `c > t`, the softmax output should be 0.0. Actually, I need to be careful. The softmax is computed over ALL T positions, but positions > t get masked out. So the denominator should sum over only the unmasked positions. A common approach: 1. Find max over unmasked positions (c <= t) 2. Compute exp(val - max) for c <= t 3. Sum exp over c <= t 4. For c <= t: out = exp(val - max) / sum 5. For c > t: out = 0.0 This is correct and efficient because we only need to reduce over c <= t. But we also need to compute the max correctly. Since all masked positions are -inf, the max is the max of unmasked positions. In our kernel: ```cuda float local_max = -1e20f; for (int c = threadIdx.x; c < T; c += blockDim.x) { float val = (c <= t) ? row_inp[c] * scale : -1e20f; local_max = fmaxf(local_max, val); } // reduce max // ... float max_val = ...; // from reduction float local_sum = 0.0f; for (int c = threadIdx.x; c < T; c += blockDim.x) { if (c <= t) { float val = row_inp[c] * scale; local_sum += expf(val - max_val); } } // reduce sum float sum_val = ...; for (int c = threadIdx.x; c < T; c += blockDim.x) { if (c <= t) { float val = row_inp[c] * scale; row_out[c] = expf(val - max_val) / sum_val; } else { row_out[c] = 0.0f; } } ``` Actually, one optimization: since for most col > row we write 0, and the causal mask is triangular, on average only half the elements are non-zero. But for small T=256, the overhead of checking `c <= t` is fine. For block organization: one block per row. T=256 rows per head. With B=32, H=12, total rows = 32*12*256 = 98304. Each block handles one row with e.g. 128 threads. Wait, with T=256 and block size 256, each thread handles 1 column. Perfect! So blockDim.x = 256, each block one row. For sum reduction with 256 threads, we can use shared memory tree reduction. Let me write the kernel more carefully: ```cuda __global__ void fused_scaled_causal_softmax_kernel( float* out, const float* inp, float scale, int B, int H, int T ) { int row = blockIdx.x; if (row >= B * H * T) return; int t = row % T; const float* row_inp = inp + row * T; float* row_out = out + row * T; __shared__ float sdata[256]; // Step 1: find max among unmasked (c <= t) float local_max = -1e20f; for (int c = threadIdx.x; c < T; c += blockDim.x) { float val = (c <= t) ? row_inp[c] * scale : -1e20f; local_max = fmaxf(local_max, val); } sdata[threadIdx.x] = local_max; __syncthreads(); for (int stride = blockDim.x / 2; stride > 0; stride >>= 1) { if (threadIdx.x < stride) { sdata[threadIdx.x] = fmaxf(sdata[threadIdx.x], sdata[threadIdx.x + stride]); } __syncthreads(); } float row_max = sdata[0]; __syncthreads(); // Step 2: compute exp and sum float local_sum = 0.0f; for (int c = threadIdx.x; c < T; c += blockDim.x) { if (c <= t) { float val = row_inp[c] * scale; float exp_val = expf(val - row_max); sdata[threadIdx.x] = exp_val; // Wait, can't reuse sdata like this if blockDim.x > T // Actually we should store in a local array or just compute sum and write output later local_sum += exp_val; } } // Let me use a separate shared memory for sum reduction } ``` Hmm, there's a collision: I used `sdata` for max, but if I want to use it for sum too, I need to make sure it's safe. Since after reading `row_max`, we can reuse `sdata`. ```cuda // ... get row_max ... // Step 2: compute local sum float local_sum = 0.0f; for (int c = threadIdx.x; c < T; c += blockDim.x) { if (c <= t) { float val = row_inp[c] * scale; local_sum += expf(val - row_max); } } __shared__ float s_sum[256]; s_sum[threadIdx.x] = local_sum; __syncthreads(); for (int stride = blockDim.x / 2; stride > 0; stride >>= 1) { if (threadIdx.x < stride) { s_sum[threadIdx.x] += s_sum[threadIdx.x + stride]; } __syncthreads(); } float row_sum = s_sum[0]; __syncthreads(); // Step 3: write output for (int c = threadIdx.x; c < T; c += blockDim.x) { if (c <= t) { float val = row_inp[c] * scale; row_out[c] = expf(val - row_max) / row_sum; } else { row_out[c] = 0.0f; } } ``` With blockDim.x = 256 and T = 256, each thread handles exactly 1 column (no loop needed!). Even simpler: ```cuda __global__ void fused_scaled_causal_softmax_kernel(...) { int row = blockIdx.x; if (row >= B * H * T) return; int t = row % T; const float* row_inp = inp + row * T; float* row_out = out + row * T; int c = threadIdx.x; __shared__ float sdata[256]; float val = (c <= t) ? row_inp[c] * scale : -1e20f; sdata[c] = val; __syncthreads(); // max reduction for (int stride = 128; stride > 0; stride >>= 1) { if (c < stride) sdata[c] = fmaxf(sdata[c], sdata[c + stride]); __syncthreads(); } float row_max = sdata[0]; __syncthreads(); if (c <= t) { sdata[c] = expf(row_inp[c] * scale - row_max); } else { sdata[c] = 0.0f; } __syncthreads(); // sum reduction for (int stride = 128; stride > 0; stride >>= 1) { if (c < stride) sdata[c] += sdata[c + stride]; __syncthreads(); } float row_sum = sdata[0]; if (c <= t) { row_out[c] = expf(row_inp[c] * scale - row_max) / row_sum; } else { row_out[c] = 0.0f; } } ``` This is clean and efficient for T=256 with block size 256. One issue: if T > 256, this won't work. But T=256 for this task, so it's fine. I'll add a check or use a loop for generality. But since the parameter is fixed, I can hardcode 256 or make it work for any T. Actually, to be safe, I'll write it with a loop over `c = threadIdx.x; c < T; c += blockDim.x`. But then the shared memory reduction is trickier because different threads process different numbers of elements. One approach with loop: ```cuda float local_max = -1e20f; for (int c = threadIdx.x; c < T; c += blockDim.x) { float val = (c <= t) ? row_inp[c] * scale : -1e20f; local_max = fmaxf(local_max, val); } sdata[threadIdx.x] = local_max; __syncthreads(); for (int stride = blockDim.x / 2; stride > 0; stride >>= 1) { if (threadIdx.x < stride) { sdata[threadIdx.x] = fmaxf(sdata[threadIdx.x], sdata[threadIdx.x + stride]); } __syncthreads(); } float row_max = sdata[0]; __syncthreads(); float local_sum = 0.0f; for (int c = threadIdx.x; c < T; c += blockDim.x) { if (c <= t) { local_sum += expf(row_inp[c] * scale - row_max); } } sdata[threadIdx.x] = local_sum; __syncthreads(); for (int stride = blockDim.x / 2; stride > 0; stride >>= 1) { if (threadIdx.x < stride) { sdata[threadIdx.x] += sdata[threadIdx.x + stride]; } __syncthreads(); } float row_sum = sdata[0]; for (int c = threadIdx.x; c < T; c += blockDim.x) { if (c <= t) { row_out[c] = expf(row_inp[c] * scale - row_max) / row_sum; } else { row_out[c] = 0.0f; } } ``` This works for any T with blockDim.x threads. I'll use blockDim.x = 256 for good occupancy. OK now let me think about the C++ host wrappers. For `load_inline`, I need to write the C++ code as a string in Python. Actually, let me look at how the kernel code should be structured. The submission should be a complete Python file with `ModelNew` class. Here's the plan for the complete code: ```python import torch import torch.nn.functional as F from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline cuda_source = r''' #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> #include <math.h> #include <stdexcept> __global__ void fused_add_ln_kernel(...) { ... } __global__ void fused_bias_gelu_kernel(...) { ... } __global__ void fused_scaled_causal_softmax_kernel(...) { ... } torch::Tensor fused_add_ln_cuda(torch::Tensor residual, torch::Tensor delta, torch::Tensor gamma, torch::Tensor beta, float eps) { // checks auto out = torch::empty_like(residual); That would be perfect. ```cuda __global__ void fused_add_ln_dual_kernel( float* out_ln, float* out_add, const float* residual, const float* delta, ... ) { // compute x = residual + delta // write x to out_add // compute ln(x) and write to out_ln } ``` This is very efficient! One kernel launch produces both: - The residual for future use - The normalized input for the next submodule So for each transformer block: 1. `h_ln = layer_norm_1(h)` using PyTorch `F.layer_norm` 2. `attn_out = attention(h_ln)` 3. `fused_add_ln_dual(h_mid, mlp_input, h, attn_out, ln_2_w, ln_2_b)` - `h_mid = h + attn_out` - `mlp_input = ln_2(h_mid)` 4. `mlp_out = mlp(mlp_input)` 5. `h = h_mid + mlp_out` For the last layer, instead of step 5, after MLP: ```python h = h_mid + mlp_out h = final_ln(h) ``` Wait, I can also fuse step 5 with the next layer's `ln_1`! For layer i where i < num_layers - 1: After MLP, we have `h_next = h_mid + mlp_out`. Then layer i+1 needs `ln_1(h_next)`. Actually, the pattern for layers 1 to N-1 would be: ```python # After MLP of previous layer: # h = ln_1_current(h_prev_mid + mlp_out_prev) # But wait, ln_1 is specific to each layer. ``` Let me think about the data flow again: **Layer 0:** - Input: `h0` (embeddings) - `attn_input0 = ln_1_0(h0)` - `attn_out0 = attn(attn_input0)` - `h_mid0 = h0 + attn_out0` - `mlp_input0 = ln_2_0(h_mid0)` - `mlp_out0 = mlp(mlp_input0)` **Layer 1:** - Input: `h1 = h_mid0 + mlp_out0` - `attn_input1 = ln_1_1(h1)` - `attn_out1 = attn(attn_input1)` - `h_mid1 = h1 + attn_out1` - `mlp_input1 = ln_2_1(h_mid1)` - `mlp_out1 = mlp(mlp_input1)` **...** **Layer N-1:** - Input: `h_{N-1} = h_mid_{N-2} + mlp_out_{N-2}` - `attn_input_{N-1} = ln_1_{N-1}(h_{N-1})` - `attn_out_{N-1} = attn(attn_input_{N-1})` - `h_mid_{N-1} = h_{N-1} + attn_out_{N-1}` - `mlp_input_{N-1} = ln_2_{N-1}(h_mid_{N-1})` - `mlp_out_{N-1} = mlp(mlp_input_{N-1})` - `h_N = h_mid_{N-1} + mlp_out_{N-1}` - `h_out = ln_f(h_N)` - `logits = h_out @ W_lm_head.T` Fusion opportunities: - `h_mid0 = h0 + attn_out0` and `mlp_input0 = ln_2_0(h_mid0)` → `fused_add_ln_dual` - `h1 = h_mid0 + mlp_out0` and `attn_input1 = ln_1_1(h1)` → `fused_add_ln_dual`! In `fused_add_ln_single`: - Input: `residual` (which is `h_mid_{i-1}`), `delta` (which is `mlp_out_{i-1}`), `gamma`, `beta` - Output: `ln(residual + delta)` - But I also need to save `h_i = residual + delta` for the attention residual! So for layer i > 0, I need: - `h_i = h_mid_{i-1} + mlp_out_{i-1}` - `attn_input_i = ln_1_i(h_i)` Then later: - `h_mid_i = h_i + attn_out_i` - `mlp_input_i = ln_2_i(h_mid_i)` I need `h_i` for both! So every transition between submodules can use `fused_add_ln_dual`: 1. After embed: `h0` is just the embeddings. Need `ln_1_0(h0)`. Since no add, can use regular LN or single fused_add_ln with delta=0. 2. After attention in layer 0: `fused_add_ln_dual(h0, attn_out0, ln_2_0_w, ln_2_0_b)` → `h_mid0`, `mlp_input0` 3. After MLP in layer 0: `fused_add_ln_dual(h_mid0, mlp_out0, ln_1_1_w, ln_1_1_b)` → `h1`, `attn_input1` 4. After attention in layer 1: `fused_add_ln_dual(h1, attn_out1, ln_2_1_w, ln_2_1_b)` → `h_mid1`, `mlp_input1` 5. After MLP in layer 1: `fused_add_ln_dual(h_mid1, mlp_out1, ln_1_2_w, ln_1_2_b)` → `h2`, `attn_input2` ... After last MLP: `fused_add_ln_dual(h_mid_{N-1}, mlp_out_{N-1}, ln_f_w, ln_f_b)` → `h_N`, `h_out` (but we only need `h_out = ln_f(h_N)`) Wait, do we need `h_N` after the final layer norm? No, we just need `ln_f(h_N)`. So the ONLY time we need a non-dual LN is at the very beginning (before first attention). We can either: - Use PyTorch `F.layer_norm` for the first ln_1 - Or write a simple `layer_norm` kernel - Or use `fused_add_ln_dual` with delta=0... but that requires creating a zero tensor. Actually, I can write a simple `layer_norm_cuda` kernel. Or just use PyTorch's `F.layer_norm`. For one call at the beginning, PyTorch is fine. And at the end, the last `fused_add_ln_dual` gives us `h_out` directly, since we don't need `h_N` for anything else! But wait, `ln_f` is not the same as any `ln_1`. It's the final layer norm. And there's no extra residual before it - it's just `ln_f(h_{N-1} + mlp_out_{N-1})` where `h_{N-1}` is the input to the last layer. Actually, for the last layer's attention, we need `attn_input_{N-1} = ln_1_{N-1}(h_{N-1})`. For the last layer's MLP, we need `mlp_input_{N-1} = ln_2_{N-1}(h_mid_{N-1})` where `h_mid_{N-1} = h_{N-1} + attn_out_{N-1}`. After the last MLP: ```python h_N = h_mid_{N-1} + mlp_out_{N-1} h_out = ln_f(h_N) ``` This is exactly `fused_add_ln_dual(h_mid_{N-1}, mlp_out_{N-1}, ln_f_w, ln_f_b)`! So the algorithm is: ```python h = wte[x] + wpe[pos] # [B, T, C] # First layer: need ln_1_0(h) attn_input = F.layer_norm(h, h.shape[-1:], ln_1_0_w, ln_1_0_b, 1e-5) attn_out = self._attn(attn_input, layer0) h_mid, mlp_input = custom_ops.fused_add_ln_dual(h, attn_out, ln_2_0_w, ln_2_0_b, 1e-5) mlp_out = self._mlp(mlp_input, layer0) for layer_idx in range(1, self.num_layers): layer = self.layers[layer_idx] if layer_idx < self.num_layers - 1: next_ln_1_w = self.layers[layer_idx + 1]['ln_1_w'] # Wait no! # Actually: # If layer_idx == 1: we need ln_1_1 # If layer_idx == N-1: we need ln_f # For 1 <= layer_idx <= N-1: the "single" or "dual" after MLP uses ln_1_{layer_idx} for next layer, # except for the last one which uses ln_f. # After MLP of layer_idx-1, we do dual with ln_1_{layer_idx} (or ln_f if last) # But wait, I was computing MLP first then dual. # Let me restructure: # For layer 0: # attn_out = attn(ln_1_0(h)) # h_mid, mlp_input = dual(h, attn_out, ln_2_0) # mlp_out = mlp(mlp_input) # After MLP of layer i-1: # h_i, attn_input_i = dual(h_mid_prev, mlp_out_prev, ln_1_i) for i >= 1 # attn_out_i = attn(attn_input_i) # h_mid_i, mlp_input_i = dual(h_i, attn_out_i, ln_2_i) # mlp_out_i = mlp(mlp_input_i) # After last MLP: # h_out = dual(h_mid_last, mlp_out_last, ln_f) # we only use the ln output ``` Actually, that's beautiful! For layers 1 to N-1, each iteration of the loop does: 1. `dual(h_mid_prev, mlp_out_prev, ln_1_i)` → `h_i`, `attn_input_i` 2. `attn_out_i = attention(attn_input_i)` 3. `dual(h_i, attn_out_i, ln_2_i)` → `h_mid_i`, `mlp_input_i` 4. `mlp_out_i = mlp(mlp_input_i)` For the first layer (layer 0): 1. `attn_input_0 = ln_1_0(h_0)` where `h_0` is embeddings 2. `attn_out_0 = attention(attn_input_0)` 3. `dual(h_0, attn_out_0, ln_2_0)` → `h_mid_0`, `mlp_input_0` 4. `mlp_out_0 = mlp(mlp_input_0)` After the loop, for the last layer (layer N-1), we don't need to compute the dual with ln_1_N because there is no layer N. Instead: 5. `dual(h_mid_{N-1}, mlp_out_{N-1}, ln_f)` → `h_N`, `h_out` And then we just use `h_out`. For N=12: - Layer 0: standalone ln_1_0, attn, dual with ln_2_0, mlp - Layers 1..11: dual with ln_1_i, attn, dual with ln_2_i, mlp - After layer 11: dual with ln_f This uses: - 1 standalone LN (PyTorch) - 12 attn calls - 12 dual with ln_2 - 11 dual with ln_1 - 1 dual with ln_f - Total dual calls: 24 If we did it naively without fusion: - 12 ln_1 - 12 attn - 12 add (residual after attn) - 12 ln_2 - 12 mlp - 12 add (residual after mlp) - 1 ln_f - Total: 61 elementwise/LN kernel calls With fusion we have 24 fused calls + 1 standalone LN. That's a big reduction. 24 dual calls means each does add + LN in one kernel instead of separate add + LN. So we save about 24 kernel launches. For the attention, if we also fuse scale + mask + softmax, we save 12*2 = 24 more kernel launches (scale, mask, softmax become 1 kernel). And for MLP, if we fuse bias + GELU, we save 12 kernel launches (bias add + gelu become 1, though PyTorch linear might already fuse bias add). Actually, PyTorch's `F.linear` (which calls `addmm`) does fuse the bias add with the GEMM for sufficiently large matrices via cublas. For [8192, 768] @ [768, 3072], cublas likely fuses it. So the standalone bias add kernel might not exist in the reference. But `gelu` is definitely a separate kernel. In our custom code, we call `torch.matmul` which does NOT include bias. Then we call `fused_bias_gelu`. So compared to `F.linear` + `gelu`, we might be replacing a potentially-fused `addmm` with `matmul` + our kernel. Hmm. Wait, actually `torch.matmul` and then `fused_bias_gelu` might not be faster than `F.linear` + `F.gelu` if `F.linear` uses a fused `addmm`. Let me think. `F.linear(input, weight, bias)` calls into `addmm` or some variant. The bias add is done either in the GEMM (if cublas supports it) or in a separate kernel. For large matrices, cublas `cublasSgemm` can do C = alpha*A*B + beta*C. So if bias is passed correctly, it's a single kernel call. But `F.linear` does `input @ weight.T + bias`. PyTorch calls `addmm(bias, input, weight.T)` which may or may not be fused depending on sizes and code paths. In practice, PyTorch is very good at fusing bias into linear via cublas. So `F.linear(x, W, b)` is likely just one GEMM kernel. If I use `torch.matmul(x, W)` + `fused_bias_gelu(x, b)`, my `matmul` is one cublas kernel, and `fused_bias_gelu` is one custom kernel. The reference has one cublas kernel for `F.linear(x, W, b)` and one kernel for `gelu`. So I save one kernel launch (the bias add and gelu are fused). But more importantly, I save memory bandwidth because `fused_bias_gelu` reads the matmul output from memory, adds bias, applies GELU, and writes back. The reference would have `F.linear` write to memory, then `gelu` reads and writes memory. If `F.linear` is fused, it still writes the result once, then `gelu` reads it. So my `fused_bias_gelu` saves one memory read/write of the intermediate. That's good. Actually wait - `torch.matmul(x, W)` outputs a tensor. Then `fused_bias_gelu` reads that tensor and writes to a new tensor. That's 1 read + 1 write. The reference: `F.linear` writes to a tensor (1 write of the output, but it had to compute it so roughly same compute). Then `gelu` reads the output and writes to a new tensor (1 read + 1 write). If `F.linear` and `gelu` are separate kernels, the reference has 1 write + 1 read + 1 write = more memory traffic. Actually, in our fused approach: `matmul` writes `fc_output` (1 write), then `fused_bias_gelu` reads `fc_output` and writes `act_output` (1 read + 1 write). Total: 2 writes + 1 read. Reference: `F.linear` writes `fc_output` (1 write), `gelu` reads `fc_output` and writes `act_output` (1 read + 1 write). Total: 2 writes + 1 read. If `F.linear` is fused with bias, then it's 1 write + 1 read + 1 write = same. So the memory bandwidth might be similar. The main win is reducing kernel launch overhead. For the attention softmax: reference does scale (elementwise), mask (elementwise), softmax (reduction + elementwise). That's 3 kernel launches. We do 1. And we save memory bandwidth by not writing intermediates. For the add + LN: reference does add (elementwise) and ln (reduction + elementwise). That's 2 kernel launches. We do 1. Saves memory bandwidth. These fusions are definitely worthwhile. Now, for accuracy, I need to make sure my layer norm matches PyTorch's exactly. PyTorch LayerNorm: ```python mean = x.mean(dim=-1, keepdim=True) var = x.var(dim=-1, keepdim=True, unbiased=False) # biased variance! out = (x - mean) / sqrt(var + eps) * gamma + beta ``` So `var = mean(x^2) - mean(x)^2`, which is the biased population variance. In my kernel, I computed: ```cpp float mean = sum / C; float var = sq_sum / C - mean * mean; ``` That's exactly the biased variance. Good. And `eps = 1e-5` for GPT2 layer norms. Let me now think about potential numerical issues. ### Attention Numerical Equivalence The reference attention does: 1. `query, key, value = self.c_attn(hidden_states).split(self.split_size, dim=2)` - For GPT2, `c_attn` projects to `3 * hidden_size`. So output is [B, T, 2304], then split into 3 [B, T, 768] tensors. 2. `query = self._split_heads(query, self.num_heads, self.head_dim)` - Reshapes [B, T, 768] to [B, num_heads, T, head_dim] = [B, 12, T, 64] - Actually the exact reshape might be different. Let me check. In `_split_heads`: ```python x = x.view(new_x_shape) return x.permute(0, 2, 1, 3) ``` Where `new_x_shape = x.size()[:-1] + (num_heads, head_dim)`. So [B, T, 768] → [B, T, 12, 64]. Then permute to [B, 12, T, 64]. Key and value do the same. 3. `attn_weights = torch.matmul(query, key.transpose(-1, -2))` - [B, 12, T, 64] @ [B, 12, 64, T] = [B, 12, T, T] 4. `attn_weights = attn_weights / (float(value.size(-1)) ** 0.5)` - Divide by 8 5. `attn_weights = torch.where(self.bias, attn_weights, self.masked_bias)` - `self.bias` is [1, 1, max_pos, max_pos], lower triangular bool mask - For eval with T=256, it slices to [1, 1, T, T] = lower triangular - `self.masked_bias` is scalar `-1e4` - For False positions (upper triangular), set to `-1e4` 6. `attn_weights = nn.functional.softmax(attn_weights, dim=-1)` 7. `attn_output = torch.matmul(attn_weights, value)` - [B, 12, T, T] @ [B, 12, T, 64] = [B, 12, T, 64] 8. `attn_output = self._merge_heads(attn_output)` - `attn_output.permute(0, 2, 1, 3).contiguous()` gives [B, T, 12, 64] - `.view(new_x_shape)` where `new_x_shape = attn_output.size()[:-2] + (num_heads * head_dim,)` gives [B, T, 768] Then `attn_output = self.c_proj(attn_output)` which is another linear layer. So in my attention function, I need to match all of this. For the softmax, I should use a large negative number like `-1e4` to match the reference. One issue: the reference might use `masked_bias = -1e4` but during causal masking, positions where `j > i` get this value. Then softmax computes `exp(-1e4)` which underflows to 0 in float32. This is fine. Actually, let me make sure about the exact value. Given fp32 softmax, even -100 gives exp(-100) ≈ 3.7e-44 which rounds to 0 in float32. So -1e4 is more than sufficient. Actually, the softmax of a row might have max value at some position. For positions before the mask, the values are normal. For masked positions, -1e4 is way less than the max, so exp(-1e4 - max) underflows to 0. So the mask value doesn't matter as long as it's large negative. But I should use exactly the same to be safe. -10000.0 seems likely. For softmax numerical stability, I'll do the standard: 1. Find max over unmasked positions in the row 2. Subtract max, exp, sum 3. Divide This matches PyTorch's softmax exactly. ### GELU Activation As I noted, GPT2 uses `gelu_new` in many versions. Let me verify by actually checking what happens in the model. But since I can't run code right now to inspect the config, I'll assume `gelu_new` is used. Wait, the task might use a specific version of transformers. Since the model is loaded from pretrained `"gpt2"`, the config is loaded from the hub. Let me think about what `activation_function` is for the canonical `gpt2` model. Looking at the `config.json` for `gpt2` on HuggingFace: ```json { "activation_function": "gelu_new", ... } ``` Yes, it's `gelu_new`. So I must use: ```python 0.5 * x * (1.0 + tanh(sqrt(2.0 / pi) * (x + 0.044715 * x^3))) ``` In CUDA: ```cpp inline float gelu_new(float x) { return 0.5f * x * (1.0f + tanhf(0.7978845608f * (x + 0.044715f * x * x * x))); } ``` Actually, let me be more precise with the constants: - `sqrt(2/pi)` = `0.7978845608028654` - `0.044715` I'll write them as double literals and cast to float, or just use float literals with enough precision. ```cpp const float SQRT_2_OVER_PI = 0.7978845608f; const float GELU_COEF = 0.044715f; ``` For better precision I can use `0.79788456080286535587989211986876f`. ### Attention Splitting In the reference, `self.c_attn(hidden_states)` outputs [B, T, 3*768], then `.split(768, dim=2)` gives 3 tensors of [B, T, 768]. In my code: ```python qkv = torch.matmul(h, c_attn_w) + c_attn_b # [B, T, 3*C] q, k, v = qkv.split(C, dim=-1) ``` Where `C = 768`. Then reshape each: ```python q = q.view(B, T, H, D).permute(0, 2, 1, 3) # [B, H, T, D] k = k.view(B, T, H, D).permute(0, 2, 1, 3) v = v.view(B, T, H, D).permute(0, 2, 1, 3) ``` Wait, permute(0, 2, 1, 3) on [B, T, H, D] gives [B, H, T, D]. Yes. ### Output Projection After attention merge, we have [B, T, C]. Then `c_proj`: ```python attn_out = torch.matmul(merged, c_proj_w) + c_proj_b ``` Where `c_proj_w` is [C, C] for attention output projection. ### Structure of the Forward Pass Let me now outline the complete forward function: ```python def forward(self, x): B, T = x.shape device = x.device C = self.config.hidden_size # 768 H = self.config.num_attention_heads # 12 D = C // H # 64 # Embeddings positions = torch.arange(T, device=device) h = self.wte[x] + self.wpe[positions] # [B, T, C] # Layer 0 layer = self.layers[0] h_attn = F.layer_norm(h, h.shape[-1:], layer['ln_1_g'], layer['ln_1_b'], 1e-5) # Attention qkv = torch.matmul(h_attn, layer['attn_c_attn_w']) + layer['attn_c_attn_b'] q, k, v = qkv.split(C, dim=-1) q = q.view(B, T, H, D).permute(0, 2, 1, 3) k = k.view(B, T, H, D).permute(0, 2, 1, 3) v = v.view(B, T, H, D).permute(0, 2, 1, 3) scores = torch.matmul(q, k.transpose(-1, -2)) attn_probs = custom_ops.fused_scaled_causal_softmax_cuda(scores, 1.0 / math.sqrt(D)) attn_out = torch.matmul(attn_probs, v) attn_out = attn_out.permute(0, 2, 1, 3).reshape(B, T, C) attn_out = torch.matmul(attn_out, layer['attn_c_proj_w']) + layer['attn_c_proj_b'] # Residual + LN for MLP h_mid, mlp_input = custom_ops.fused_add_ln_dual_cuda(h, attn_out, layer['ln_2_g'], layer['ln_2_b'], 1e-5) # MLP fc_out = torch.matmul(mlp_input, layer['mlp_c_fc_w']) + layer['mlp_c_fc_b'] act_out = custom_ops.fused_bias_gelu_cuda(fc_out, layer['mlp_c_fc_b']) # Wait! fc_out already has bias added via + layer['mlp_c_fc_b']. # But in fused_bias_gelu, I also add bias! That would be double bias. ``` Oh no, I made a critical error. In the `fused_bias_gelu` kernel, I add bias. But if I also add bias in the `torch.matmul(..., W) + b`, then I'm double-counting! I need to be careful. The `fused_bias_gelu` kernel should either: 1. Take just the matmul output (without bias) and add bias inside the kernel, OR 2. Take the matmul+bias output and just apply GELU. Option 2 is simpler! I can call it `fused_gelu_cuda` and it just applies gelu elementwise. The matmul + bias is done by `torch.matmul(mlp_input, W) + b` in PyTorch, which might or might not fuse. But I wanted to fuse bias add + GELU because `matmul(x, W) + b` writes a tensor, then GELU reads it. If I fuse them, I avoid writing an intermediate. However, if I call `torch.matmul(x, W) + b`, PyTorch evaluates this and produces a tensor. Then `fused_gelu` just reads it and applies GELU. The memory bandwidth is: `matmul` writes output (1 write), `+ b` might be fused or might read+write (if not fused). Then `gelu` reads+writes. Total: potentially 2-3 passes. A `fused_bias_gelu` that takes `matmul_output` and `bias`: reads `matmul_output` and `bias`, writes `gelu(output + bias)`. That's 1 read + 1 write. Compared to `matmul` (1 write) + `add_bias` (1 read + 1 write) + `gelu` (1 read + 1 write): we save 2 memory passes. But `torch.matmul(x, W)` cannot be fused with our custom kernel because `matmul` is a separate cublas launch. So: - Option A: `fc = torch.matmul(h, W)`; `act = fused_bias_gelu(fc, b)` - matmul: reads x, W; writes fc - fused_bias_gelu: reads fc, b; writes act - Total memory: read x,W,fc,b; write fc,act - Option B: `fc = torch.matmul(h, W) + b`; `act = fused_gelu(fc)` - matmul + bias: might be fused in cublas (reads x,W,b; writes fc) - gelu: reads fc; writes act - Total memory: reads x,W,b,fc; write fc,act Actually option A might have slightly more memory traffic because `fc` is both written and read. In option B, if cublas fuses the bias add, then `fc` is only written by cublas and then read by gelu - same as option A. Wait, in option A: `fc` is written by matmul and then read by fused_bias_gelu. So `fc` has 1 write + 1 read. In option B with fused cublas: cublas writes `fc`, then gelu reads `fc`. Also 1 write + 1 read. So they're the same in terms of `fc` memory traffic. The difference is that in option A, the bias is read by our kernel simultaneously with `fc`. In option B, bias is read by cublas during the GEMM. Since cublas is very efficient at reading bias during GEMM, option B might actually be better if cublas fuses it. But it's not guaranteed. However, for our custom kernel, if we want to call it `fused_bias_gelu`, we need to pass the bias explicitly. But then we should NOT add bias in the matmul call. So: ```python fc = torch.matmul(mlp_input, W) # no bias act = custom_ops.fused_bias_gelu(fc, b) ``` This is option A. And `fc` is written by matmul and read by our kernel. Wait, but there's a subtle issue: in the reference, `c_fc` and `c_proj` are `Conv1D` layers which have bias. And `c_attn` also has bias. If I use `F.linear`, it includes bias. Let me go with two device kernels. ### CUDA Kernel - fused_add_ln_dual ```cuda __global__ void fused_add_ln_dual_kernel( float* add_out, float* ln_out, const float* residual, const float* delta, const float* gamma, const float* beta, int B, int T, int C, float eps ) { int bt = blockIdx.x; int b = bt / T; int t = bt % T; if (b >= B) return; int offset = (b * T + t) * C; const float* r_ptr = residual + offset; const float* d_ptr = delta + offset; float* a_ptr = add_out + offset; float* l_ptr = ln_out + offset; extern __shared__ float sdata[]; float* s_sum = sdata; float* s_sq = sdata + blockDim.x; // Each thread computes partial sum float thread_sum = 0.0f; float thread_sq = 0.0f; for (int c = threadIdx.x; c < C; c += blockDim.x) { float x = r_ptr[c] + d_ptr[c]; thread_sum += x; thread_sq += x * x; } s_sum[threadIdx.x] = thread_sum; s_sq[threadIdx.x] = thread_sq; __syncthreads(); // Tree reduction for (int stride = blockDim.x / 2; stride > 0; stride >>= 1) { if (threadIdx.x < stride) { s_sum[threadIdx.x] += s_sum[threadIdx.x + stride]; s_sq[threadIdx.x] += s_sq[threadIdx.x + stride]; } __syncthreads(); } float mean = s_sum[0] / C; float var = s_sq[0] / C - mean * mean; float inv_std = rsqrtf(var + eps); __syncthreads(); // Write outputs for (int c = threadIdx.x; c < C; c += blockDim.x) { float x = r_ptr[c] + d_ptr[c]; a_ptr[c] = x; l_ptr[c] = (x - mean) * inv_std * gamma[c] + beta[c]; } } ``` Wait, shared memory size. I'm using `s_sum` and `s_sq` each of size `blockDim.x`. If blockDim.x=256, that's 256*4*2 = 2048 bytes. Plus there might not be a conflict since they're in `extern __shared__`. But I need to declare it properly. Actually, since they don't overlap, I can just do: ```cuda extern __shared__ float sdata[]; float* s_sum = sdata; float* s_sq = sdata + blockDim.x; ``` And in the kernel launch, shared memory size = `2 * blockDim.x * sizeof(float)`. Or I can just use `__shared__ float s_sum[256]; __shared__ float s_sq[256];` and ensure `blockDim.x <= 256`. Since I'll always use 256 threads, this is fine and simpler. In fact, I'll use fixed size: ```cuda __shared__ float s_sum[256]; __shared__ float s_sq[256]; ``` And assert `blockDim.x <= 256` or just always launch with 256 threads. For C=768 and 256 threads, each thread handles 3 elements, which is fine. For generality if C changes, using a loop is good. But for GPT2 small, C=768. To make the kernel work for any C <= 768*something, I'll use loops and fixed shared memory of 256. ### CUDA Kernel - fused_add_ln (single output) ```cuda __global__ void fused_add_ln_kernel( float* ln_out, const float* residual, const float* delta, const float* gamma, const float* beta, int B, int T, int C, float eps ) { // Same as above but doesn't write add_out } ``` ### CUDA Kernel - fused_bias_gelu ```cuda __global__ void fused_bias_gelu_kernel( float* out, const float* inp, const float* bias, int B, int T, int C ) { int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int total = B * T * C; if (idx >= total) return; int c = idx % C; float x = inp[idx] + bias[c]; out[idx] = 0.5f * x * (1.0f + tanhf(0.7978845608f * (x + 0.044715f * x * x * x))); } ``` Actually, wait - `0.044715f * x * x * x` might overflow if x is large. But for GPT2 activations, x is typically in reasonable range. The tanh formula is the standard `gelu_new`. ### Host Wrappers I need to write C++ host wrapper functions. Here's what `load_inline` expects: ```cpp torch::Tensor fused_add_ln_cuda(torch::Tensor residual, torch::Tensor delta, ...) { auto out = torch::empty_like(residual); int B = residual.size(0); int T = residual.size(1); int C = residual.size(2); int blocks = B * T; int threads = 256; fused_add_ln_kernel<<<blocks, threads>>>(...); return out; } std::vector<torch::Tensor> fused_add_ln_dual_cuda(...) { auto add_out = torch::empty_like(residual); auto ln_out = torch::empty_like(residual); ... return {add_out, ln_out}; } torch::Tensor fused_bias_gelu_cuda(torch::Tensor input, torch::Tensor bias) { auto out = torch::empty_like(input); int B = input.size(0); int T = input.size(1); int C = input.size(2); int total = B * T * C; int threads = 256; int blocks = (total + threads - 1) / threads; fused_bias_gelu_kernel<<<blocks, threads>>>(...); return out; } torch::Tensor fused_scaled_causal_softmax_cuda(torch::Tensor input, float scale) { auto out = torch::empty_like(input); int B = input.size(0); int H = input.size(1); int T = input.size(2); int rows = B * H * T; int threads = 256; fused_scaled_causal_softmax_kernel<<<rows, threads>>>(...); return out; } ``` Wait, for `fused_scaled_causal_softmax`, the input is 4D [B, H, T, T]. In my kernel, I flatten it to [B*H*T, T]. For `fused_bias_gelu`, the input is [B, T, C]. I flatten to 1D for indexing. One important thing: I need to make sure all tensors are contiguous and of type float32. Also, I should check CUDA errors. For simplicity, I'll add `TORCH_CHECK` for shapes and types. ### Loading Weights In `ModelNew.__init__`, I need to load the reference model and extract all weights. Since this is done once at initialization, the overhead is fine. So `lm_head.weight` might be the same tensor as `wte.weight`. In my `ModelNew`, I can just store `self.wte` and `self.lm_head_w` as the same tensor or different. Also, I need to make sure `self.config` is set if the reference sets it, because `get_init_inputs()` returns `[model_name, config]` and we need to call `ModelNew(model_name, config)`. ### Python Forward Implementation Let me draft the forward more carefully: ```python def forward(self, x): B, T = x.shape C = self.n_embd # 768 H = self.n_head # 12 D = C // H # 64 device = x.device # Embedding positions = torch.arange(T, device=device) h = self.wte[x] + self.wpe[positions] # [B, T, C] # Process first layer # For first layer: regular LN h_attn = torch.nn.functional.layer_norm(h, (C,), self.layers[0].ln_1_g, self.layers[0].ln_1_b, 1e-5) # Attention attn_out = self._attn(h_attn, self.layers[0], B, H, T, D) # Dual: h + attn_out, ln_2 h_mid, mlp_input = custom_ops.fused_add_ln_dual_cuda(h, attn_out, self.layers[0].ln_2_g, self.layers[0].ln_2_b, 1e-5) # MLP mlp_out = self._mlp(mlp_input, self.layers[0], B, T, C) # Layers 1 to N-1 for i in range(1, self.n_layer): layer = self.layers[i] prev_layer = self.layers[i-1] # Not needed # Fuse: h_mid + mlp_out then ln_1 if i < self.n_layer - 1: # regular dual with next layer's ln_1 h_i, attn_input = custom_ops.fused_add_ln_dual_cuda(h_mid, mlp_out, layer.ln_1_g, layer.ln_1_b, 1e-5) else: # For the last layer, we only need ln_1, not the add result attn_input = custom_ops.fused_add_ln_cuda(h_mid, mlp_out, layer.ln_1_g, layer.ln_1_b, 1e-5) h_i = None # Not needed attn_out = self._attn(attn_input, layer, B, H, T, D) h_mid, mlp_input = custom_ops.fused_add_ln_dual_cuda(attn_input if h_i is None else h_i, attn_out, layer.ln_2_g, layer.ln_2_b, 1e-5) mlp_out = self._mlp(mlp_input, layer, B, T, C) # After last layer: final ln h_out = custom_ops.fused_add_ln_cuda(h_mid, mlp_out, self.ln_f_g, self.ln_f_b, 1e-5) # LM head logits = torch.matmul(h_out, self.lm_head_w.t()) return logits ``` Wait, I have a bug. In the `fused_add_ln_dual`, the first argument is `residual` (the thing we add to), and the second is `delta` (the thing being added). For layer i > 0: `h_i = h_mid_{i-1} + mlp_out_{i-1}` (this is the input to layer i) `attn_input_i = ln_1_i(h_i)` So `fused_add_ln_dual(h_mid_prev, mlp_out_prev, ln_1_i_g, ln_1_i_b, eps)` → `(h_i, attn_input_i)`. Then `_attn(attn_input_i)` returns `attn_out_i`. Then `fused_add_ln_dual(h_i, attn_out_i, ln_2_i_g, ln_2_i_b, eps)` → `(h_mid_i, mlp_input_i)`. Wait, but `h_i` is only needed if we're going to use it in the next dual. For the attention residual + mlp ln, the residual is `h_i` not `h_mid_prev`. So yes, `h_i` must be passed. And for the MLP output, `mlp_out_i` is computed. Then for the next layer, we need `h_mid_i + mlp_out_i` → `h_{i+1}`. So we need `h_mid_i` from the second dual. Good. For the last layer (i = N-1), we still compute `h_mid_{N-1}` and `mlp_input_{N-1}`. Then after MLP, we need `ln_f(h_mid_{N-1} + mlp_out_{N-1})`. This uses `fused_add_ln(h_mid_{N-1}, mlp_out_{N-1}, ln_f_g, ln_f_b, eps)`. And for the last layer, we don't need `h_N = h_mid_{N-1} + mlp_out_{N-1}` for anything except the LN. So using `fused_add_ln` (single output) is correct. Similarly, for the first layer, `h_0` is the embeddings, and we need `ln_1_0(h_0)`. Since there's no previous MLP to fuse, we use either `fused_add_ln(h_0, zeros, ...)` or just PyTorch `F.layer_norm`. Using PyTorch is simpler. OK, so for the intermediate layers, the dual outputs are: - First dual in loop: outputs `(h_i, attn_input_i)` — both needed - Second dual in loop: outputs `(h_mid_i, mlp_input_i)` — both needed For layer N-1 (last layer): - Before attention: we need `h_{N-1}` and `attn_input_{N-1}`. Both needed. - After attention: we need `h_mid_{N-1}` and `mlp_input_{N-1}`. Both needed. - After MLP: we need `h_out = ln_f(h_mid_{N-1} + mlp_out_{N-1})`. Single output. For layer 0: - Before attention: `attn_input_0 = ln_1_0(h_0)`. We only need `attn_input_0`. `h_0` is already known. - After attention: we need `h_mid_0` and `mlp_input_0`. Both needed. So the structure is: ```python h = embeddings # Layer 0 attn_input = F.layer_norm(h, (C,), layers[0].ln_1_g, layers[0].ln_1_b, 1e-5) attn_out = _attn(attn_input, layers[0]) h_mid, mlp_input = dual(h, attn_out, layers[0].ln_2_g, layers[0].ln_2_b, 1e-5) mlp_out = _mlp(mlp_input, layers[0]) for i in range(1, n_layers): layer = layers[i] # Fuse previous MLP residual with current ln_1 h_i, attn_input = dual(h_mid, mlp_out, layer.ln_1_g, layer.ln_1_b, 1e-5) attn_out = _attn(attn_input, layer) h_mid, mlp_input = dual(h_i, attn_out, layer.ln_2_g, layer.ln_2_b, 1e-5) mlp_out = _mlp(mlp_input, layer) # Final h_out = single_ln(h_mid, mlp_out, ln_f_g, ln_f_b, 1e-5) logits = h_out @ lm_head_w.T ``` This is elegant and maximally fused. Let me also consider whether I can store the layer parameters more efficiently. Python looping over a list of dicts and accessing `layer['ln_1_g']` each time might add some Python overhead, but it's likely negligible compared to the kernel launches. I could use a namedtuple or just store as attributes, but not needed. ### precomputing ln_1 in the first layer For layer 0, I use `F.layer_norm`. This will call PyTorch's kernel. That's fine. One thing: `F.layer_norm` might create a new tensor. For the first layer, h is the embedding output. We can just do `attn_input = F.layer_norm(h, ...)`. ### Shape and Type Checking I need to ensure all operations produce float32 outputs. The input `x` is int64 (token IDs). `wte[x]` returns float32 (assuming wte is float32). The model weights should be float32 by default. ### The `_attn` helper I'll write a helper function for attention: ```python def _attn(self, hidden_states, layer, B, H, T, D): qkv = torch.matmul(hidden_states, layer['attn_c_attn_w']) q, k, v = qkv.split(self.n_embd, dim=-1) q = q.view(B, T, H, D).permute(0, 2, 1, 3).contiguous() k = k.view(B, T, H, D).permute(0, 2, 1, 3).contiguous() v = v.view(B, T, H, D).permute(0, 2, 1, 3).contiguous() scores = torch.matmul(q, k.transpose(-1, -2)) attn_probs = custom_ops.fused_scaled_causal_softmax_cuda(scores, 1.0 / math.sqrt(D)) attn_output = torch.matmul(attn_probs, v) attn_output = attn_output.permute(0, 2, 1, 3).reshape(B, T, self.n_embd) attn_output = torch.matmul(attn_output, layer['attn_c_proj_w']) + layer['attn_c_proj_b'] return attn_output ``` Wait, `qkv.split(self.n_embd, dim=-1)` — does this create 3 tensors of shape [B, T, 768]? Yes. But `view` and `permute` might create non-contiguous tensors. Then `torch.matmul(q, k.transpose(-1, -2))` might need to handle non-contiguous inputs. PyTorch matmul handles non-contiguous but might be slightly slower. For correctness, it should be fine. Actually, in the reference, the split produces contiguous tensors? `split` returns contiguous slices of the split dimension. Since qkv is [B, T, 2304], splitting on dim=-1 gives three [B, T, 768] tensors that are contiguous. Then `view` and `permute` create non-contiguous tensors, but `torch.matmul` handles this. However, for my custom softmax kernel, the input `scores` must be contiguous? In my kernel, I access `inp + row * T` which assumes contiguous last dimension. If `scores` is contiguous, then `scores.view(-1, T)` is valid and the kernel works. But `scores` from `matmul` should be contiguous. Wait, `scores` is [B, H, T, T]. Is the last dimension contiguous? Yes, `torch.matmul` of contiguous inputs produces a contiguous output in the leading dimensions. For [B, H, T, D] @ [B, H, D, T], the output is [B, H, T, T] and the last dimension T is contiguous. My kernel accesses `inp + ((b * H + h) * T + t) * T + c`. This assumes the tensor is contiguous and stored in row-major order. For a 4D tensor of shape [B, H, T, T], the flat index of (b, h, t, c) is indeed `((b * H + h) * T + t) * T + c`. So as long as the tensor is contiguous, my kernel is correct. I should add a check or make it contiguous: `scores = scores.contiguous()`. But `torch.matmul` should produce contiguous. I'll add `.contiguous()` just to be safe. Actually, wait — `q` and `k` are non-contiguous after `permute`. Does `torch.matmul` on non-contiguous tensors produce contiguous results? Yes, `matmul` produces a fresh contiguous tensor. For `v`, after permute it's non-contiguous, but `matmul(attn_probs, v)` will produce contiguous output. And `attn_output.permute(0, 2, 1, 3)` is non-contiguous. The `.reshape(B, T, C)` on a non-contiguous tensor might fail! I need `.reshape` to work. In PyTorch, `.reshape` works like `.view` if possible, otherwise copies. A safer approach: `.contiguous().view(B, T, C)` or just `.reshape(B*T, C)` if needed. In the reference, `_merge_heads` does: ```python x = x.permute(0, 2, 1, 3).contiguous() new_x_shape = x.size()[:-2] + (x.size(-2) * x.size(-1),) return x.view(new_x_shape) ``` So it explicitly calls `.contiguous()` before `.view()`. I should do the same: ```python attn_output = attn_output.permute(0, 2, 1, 3).contiguous().view(B, T, self.n_embd) ``` ### The `_mlp` helper ```python def _mlp(self, hidden_states, layer, B, T, C): fc = torch.matmul(hidden_states, layer['mlp_c_fc_w']) act = custom_ops.fused_bias_gelu_cuda(fc, layer['mlp_c_fc_b']) proj = torch.matmul(act, layer['mlp_c_proj_w']) + layer['mlp_c_proj_b'] return proj ``` Note: PyTorch's `torch.matmul` for [B, T, C] @ [C, 4C] gives [B, T, 4C]. For `Conv1D` weights, `mlp_c_fc_w` is [768, 3072]. So `matmul` gives [B, T, 3072]. Good. ### `fused_bias_gelu` kernel The input `fc` is [B, T, 4*C]. Wait, C=768, so 4C=3072. The kernel loops over `total = B * T * 3072` elements. `bias` is [3072]. `c = idx % C` where C=3072. Good. ### Checking `fused_scaled_causal_softmax` with scale The scale factor is `1.0 / sqrt(D)`. `D = 64`, so `sqrt(64) = 8`, scale = 0.125. It does NOT scale AND mask in a specific order that matters because the masked positions get overwritten before softmax. My kernel: for `c > t`, I set to `-10000.0f * scale`? That's correct! Masked positions get -10000.0f directly. But hold on - is the causal mask boolean applied to the pre-scaled or post-scaled values? In the reference, it's definitely post-scaled. The mask value is a constant `-1e4` that overwrites whatever computed value was there. Since those positions are set to the mask value, it doesn't matter what the computed value was. But in my kernel, I check `c <= t` BEFORE scaling. For `c <= t`, I scale. For `c > t`, I use the mask value. This is correct and equivalent. One subtlety: the reference uses `self.bias` which is a lower-triangular boolean mask where `True` means KEEP and `False` means MASK. And `self.masked_bias` might not be exactly `-1e4`. In newer `transformers`, the masking uses `torch.finfo(attn_weights.dtype).min`! This is the minimum value for the dtype. For float32, this is approximately `-3.4028235e+38`. Oh wow, this is very important. So the mask value is not `-1e4` but the actual minimum float value (`-inf`-like). If I use `-1e4` in my kernel and the reference uses `-3.4e38`, will softmax give the same result? For softmax, if the minimum value is `-1e4` and the actual values are around 0-10, then `exp(-1e4 - max)` underflows to 0. So the result is 0 for masked positions, same as if the mask value was `-3.4e38`. As long as the mask value is much smaller than the actual values minus the max, softmax will give 0. And since `-1e4` is definitely much smaller than any reasonable value, both will underflow to 0. But wait — when computing the max of the row, if we include masked positions: - Reference: max over all positions, but masked positions are -3.4e38, so the max is over unmasked positions. - My kernel: if I only look at unmasked positions for the max (by initializing with -inf and only considering c <= t), the max is over unmasked positions. If instead I set masked positions to -1e4 and include them in the max computation: - If all unmasked values are, say, -100, then max would be -1e4 if we include masked positions. But in causal attention, unmasked positions include the diagonal, so there are always valid positions. And values are usually around 0 (since Q and K are normalized). So max won't be -1e4. Actually, in my kernel design above, I only iterate `c <= t` for max and sum. I explicitly exclude masked positions. This is correct regardless of the mask value. So the mask value only matters for the intermediate softmax denominator, but since masked positions are excluded from the sum, the mask value doesn't matter at all! As long as I only compute softmax over `c <= t`, I'm fine. This means my kernel is robust to the exact mask value used. I can use -1e4 or whatever, but since I only compute over unmasked positions with the causal condition, it doesn't matter. Wait, but in my kernel above, I wrote: ```cpp float val = (c <= t) ? row_inp[c] * scale : -1e20f; ``` And then I include all positions in the max reduction but masked positions have large negative values. That's also fine. But actually, to be most correct: I can just initialize `local_max = -1e20f`, and in the loop over `c < T`, only update `local_max` when `c <= t`. That's what I planned to do. So the mask value `-1e20f` is just for shared memory initialization. Since I only reduce over the valid positions (c <= t), the exact "masked" value doesn't matter. Good. But wait, I just want to double check: in my earlier kernel draft, I had: ```cpp for (int c = threadIdx.x; c < T; c += blockDim.x) { float val = (c <= t) ? row_inp[c] * scale : -1e20f; local_max = fmaxf(local_max, val); } ``` Then: ```cpp for (int c = threadIdx.x; c < T; c += blockDim.x) { if (c <= t) { local_sum += expf(row_inp[c] * scale - row_max); } } ``` Notice in the second loop I don't include the masked positions in the sum. This is correct. And in the first loop, I include them but they're -1e20f so they don't affect max. This works fine. The result only depends on the unmasked positions. ### Attention Mask Value in Reference Actually, the critical thing is: the reference might also apply an `attention_mask` for padding.
